<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It should have been easy for the Court to say goodbye to <em>Auer</em> v. <em>Robbins</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 1" name="FRopinion1.1" href="#FNopinion1.1">1</a></sup>  In disputes involving the relationship between the government and the people, <em>Auer</em> requires judges to accept an executive agency's interpretation of its own regulations even when that interpretation doesn't represent the best and fairest reading.  This rule creates a "systematic judicial bias in favor of the federal government, the most powerful of parties, and against everyone else."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 2" name="FRopinion1.2" href="#FNopinion1.2">2</a></sup>  Nor is <em>Auer</em>'s biased rule the product of some congressional mandate we are powerless to correct: This Court invented it, almost by accident and without any meaningful effort to reconcile it with the Administrative Procedure Act or the Constitution.  A legion of academics, lower court judges, and Members of this Court--even <em>Auer</em>'s author--has called on us to abandon <em>Auer</em>.  Yet today a bare majority flinches, and <em>Auer</em> lives on.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Still, today's decision is more a stay of execution than a pardon.  The Court cannot muster even five votes to say that <em>Auer</em> is lawful or wise.  Instead, a majority retains <em>Auer</em> only because of <em>stare decisis</em>.  And yet, far from standing by that precedent, the majority proceeds to impose so many new and nebulous qualifications and limitations on <em>Auer</em> that <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The Chief Justice</small></span> claims to see little practical difference between keeping it on life support in this way and overruling it entirely.  So the doctrine emerges maimed and enfeebled--in truth, zombified.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Respectfully, we owe our colleagues on the lower courts more candid and useful guidance than this.  And judges owe the people who come before them nothing less than a fair contest, where every party has an equal chance to persuade the court of its interpretation of the law's demands.  One can hope that <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span> is right, and that whether we formally overrule <em>Auer</em> or merely neuter it, the results in most cases will prove the same.  But means, not just ends, matter, and retaining even this debilitated version of <em>Auer</em> threatens to force litigants and lower courts to jump through needless and perplexing new hoops and in the process deny the people the independent judicial decisions they deserve.  All to what end?  So that we may <em>pretend</em> to abide <em>stare decisis</em>?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consider this case.  Mr. Kisor is a Marine who lost out on benefits for post-traumatic stress disorder when the court of appeals deferred to a regulatory interpretation advanced by the Department of Veterans Affairs.  The court of appeals was guilty of nothing more than faithfully following <em>Auer</em>.  But the majority today invokes <em>stare decisis</em>, of all things, to vacate that judgment and tell the court of appeals to try again using its newly retooled, multi-factored, and far less determinate version of <em>Auer</em>.  Respectfully, I would stop this business of making up excuses for judges to abdicate their job of interpreting the law, and simply allow the court of appeals to afford Mr. Kisor its best independent judgment of the law's meaning.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court's failure to be done with <em>Auer</em>, and its decision to adorn <em>Auer</em> with so many new and ambiguous limitations, all but guarantees we will have to pass this way again.  When that day comes, I hope this Court will find the nerve it lacks today and inter <em>Auer</em> at last.  Until then, I hope that our judicial colleagues on other courts will take courage from today's ruling and realize that it has transformed <em>Auer</em> into a paper tiger.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">I. How We Got Here</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Where did <em>Auer </em>come from?  Not from the Constitution, some ancient common law tradition, or even a modern statute.  Instead, it began as an unexplained aside in a decision about emergency price controls at the height of the Second World War.  Even then, the dictum sat on the shelf, little noticed, for years.  Only in the last few decades of the 20th century did lawyers and courts really begin to dust it off and shape it into the reflexive rule of deference to regulatory agencies we know today.  And they did so without ever pausing to consider whether a rule like that could be legally justified or even made sense.  <em>Auer</em> is really little more than an accident.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Before the mid-20th century, few federal agencies engaged in extensive rulemaking, and those that did rarely sought deference for their regulatory interpretations.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 3" name="FRopinion1.3" href="#FNopinion1.3">3</a></sup>  But when the question arose, this Court did not hesitate to say that judges reviewing administrative action should decide all questions of law, including questions concerning the meaning of regulations.  As Justice Brandeis put it, "[t]he inexorable safeguard which the due process clause assures is .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. that there will be opportunity for a court to determine whether the applicable rules of law .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. were observed."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 4" name="FRopinion1.4" href="#FNopinion1.4">4</a></sup>  Unsurprisingly, the government's early, longstanding, and consistent interpretation of a statute, regulation, or other legal instrument could count as powerful <em>evidence</em> of its original public meaning.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 5" name="FRopinion1.5" href="#FNopinion1.5">5</a></sup>  But courts respected executive interpretations only because and to the extent "they embodied understandings made roughly contemporaneously with .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. enactment and stably maintained and practiced since that time," not "because they were executive as such."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 6" name="FRopinion1.6" href="#FNopinion1.6">6</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Writing for four Members of the Court, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span> suggests that <em>Auer</em>'s very different approach to the interpretation of agency regulations was foreshadowed as early as this Court's 1898 decision in <em>United States</em> v. <em>Eaton</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 7" name="FRopinion1.7" href="#FNopinion1.7">7</a></sup>  <em>Ante</em>, at 7.  But this is mistaken.  The question in that case was whether Mr. Eaton's appointment as temporary vice-consul to Siam was consistent with State Department regulations.  After several pages of careful and independent legal analysis, the Court held that the regulations did authorize the appointment.  That conclusion, the Court explained, was "rendered necessary by a consideration of the text."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 8" name="FRopinion1.8" href="#FNopinion1.8">8</a></sup>  Only <em>after</em> reaching this conclusion did the Court observe that the State Department had previously adopted the same construction, noting along the way that the Department's views were "entitled to the greatest weight" and that the Court saw "no reason in this case to doubt [their] correctness."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 9" name="FRopinion1.9" href="#FNopinion1.9">9</a></sup>  <em>Eaton</em> thus simply followed the well-worn path of acknowledging that an agency's interpretation of a regulation can supply <em>evidence</em> of its meaning.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 10" name="FRopinion1.10" href="#FNopinion1.10">10</a></sup>  Nowhere did the Court even hint that it would have deferred to the State Department's views about the meaning of the law if its own independent textual analysis had not led it to the same conclusion.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;All this is borne out by the Court's later teachings in <em>Skidmore</em> v. <em>Swift &amp; Co.</em> in 1944.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 11" name="FRopinion1.11" href="#FNopinion1.11">11</a></sup>  The question there was whether the time overnight employees spent waiting to respond to fire alarms could amount to compensable overtime under the Fair Labor Standards Act.  The lower courts had held as a matter of law that it could not.  In an opinion by Justice Jackson, this Court reversed.  The Court first held, based on its own independent analysis, that "no principle of law found either in the statute or in Court decisions precludes waiting time from also being working time."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 12" name="FRopinion1.12" href="#FNopinion1.12">12</a></sup>  Only then did the Court consider "what, if any, deference courts should pay" to the views of the Administrator of the Labor Department's Wage and Hour Division.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 13" name="FRopinion1.13" href="#FNopinion1.13">13</a></sup>  And on that question the Court reaffirmed the traditional rule that an agency's interpretation of the law is "not controlling upon the courts" and is entitled only to a weight proportional to "the thoroughness evident in its consideration, the validity of its reasoning, its consistency with earlier and later pronouncements, and all those factors which give it power to persuade."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 14" name="FRopinion1.14" href="#FNopinion1.14">14</a></sup>  At the time, the influential administrative law scholar Kenneth Culp Davis considered this "[a]n entirely reliable statement" of the law.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 15" name="FRopinion1.15" href="#FNopinion1.15">15</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In truth, the seeds of the <em>Auer</em> doctrine were first planted only in 1945, in <em>Bowles</em> v. <em>Seminole Rock &amp; Sand Co.</em><sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 16" name="FRopinion1.16" href="#FNopinion1.16">16</a></sup>  That case involved regulations issued by the Office of Price Administration (OPA), which Congress had tasked with stabilizing the national economy during the Second World War through the use of emergency price controls.  It was in that context that the Court declared--for the first time and without citing any authority--that "if the meaning of [the regulation were] in doubt," the agency's interpretation would merit "controlling weight unless it is plainly erroneous or inconsistent with the regulation."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 17" name="FRopinion1.17" href="#FNopinion1.17">17</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Yet even then it was far from clear how much weight the Court really placed on the agency's interpretation.  As it had in <em>Eaton</em>, the Court in <em>Seminole Rock </em>began with an extended discussion of "the plain words of the regulation," which led it to conclude that the text "clearly" supported the government's position.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 18" name="FRopinion1.18" href="#FNopinion1.18">18</a></sup>  Only after reaching that conclusion based on its own independent analysis did the Court proceed to add that "[a]ny doubts .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. are removed by reference to the administrative construction."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 19" name="FRopinion1.19" href="#FNopinion1.19">19</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;So confused was all this that readers at the time didn't perceive <em>Seminole Rock</em>'s dictum as changing anything.  Professor Davis observed that the Court's discussion about giving "controlling weight" to the agency's interpretation was an unexplained aside that made no difference to the case's outcome.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 20" name="FRopinion1.20" href="#FNopinion1.20">20</a></sup>  The dictum, too, was readily explained as reflecting the unusual factual context in which the case arose, involving an emergency government program created to deal with "unique circumstances of war and economic depression."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 21" name="FRopinion1.21" href="#FNopinion1.21">21</a></sup>  And the Court decided <em>Seminole Rock</em> the same Term it issued <em>Skidmore</em>, where it reaffirmed the traditional rule that an agency's views about the law may <em>persuade</em> a court but can never <em>control</em> its judgment.  In fact, the Court in <em>Seminole Rock</em> was careful to note that the OPA interpretation before it bore many of the characteristics <em>Skidmore</em> would have recognized as increasing its persuasive force: It had been announced concurrently with the regulation, disseminated widely to the regulated community, and adhered to consistently by the agency.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 22" name="FRopinion1.22" href="#FNopinion1.22">22</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;No wonder, then, that for many years after the decision, courts "connected <em>Seminole Rock</em> more closely with the deference framework .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. under <em>Skidmore</em>" and generally engaged in a <em>Skidmore</em>-type analysis, accepting the agency's interpretation "only after independently examining the regulation and concluding that the agency interpretation was sound."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 23" name="FRopinion1.23" href="#FNopinion1.23">23</a></sup>  If <em>Seminole Rock</em>'s "controlling weight" dictum was afforded any force, it was usually only in the price control context; even then it was ordinarily extended only to "official" agency interpretations that were published contemporaneously with the regulation and widely distributed.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 24" name="FRopinion1.24" href="#FNopinion1.24">24</a></sup>  The Fourth Circuit exemplified the early understanding of <em>Seminole Rock</em> when it observed--citing both <em>Seminole Rock</em> and <em>Skidmore</em>--that "under settled principles" an official agency interpretation in an opinion letter was entitled only to "respectful consideration."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 25" name="FRopinion1.25" href="#FNopinion1.25">25</a></sup>  The letter, the court stressed, did not "have the effect of law," and "[i]t would be absurd to hold that the courts must subordinate their judgment as to the meaning of a .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. regulation to the mere unsupported opinion of an associate counsel in an administrative department."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 26" name="FRopinion1.26" href="#FNopinion1.26">26</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This Court did not cite <em>Seminole Rock</em>'s "controlling weight" dictum again until 1965, in <em>Udall</em> v. <em>Tallman</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 27" name="FRopinion1.27" href="#FNopinion1.27">27</a></sup>  And though <em>Tallman</em> "did very little to advance the jurisprudential understanding of <em>Seminole Rock</em>," it certainly helped fuel the expansion of so-called "<em>Seminole Rock</em> deference."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 28" name="FRopinion1.28" href="#FNopinion1.28">28</a></sup>  From the 1960s on, this Court and lower courts began to cite the <em>Seminole Rock</em> dictum with increasing frequency and in a wider variety of circumstances, but still without much explanation.  They also increasingly divorced <em>Seminole Rock</em> from <em>Skidmore</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 29" name="FRopinion1.29" href="#FNopinion1.29">29</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Auer </em>represents the apotheosis of this line of cases.  In the name of what some now call the <em>Auer</em> doctrine, courts have in recent years "mechanically applied and reflexively treated" <em>Seminole Rock</em>'s dictum "as a constraint upon the careful inquiry that one might ordinarily expect of courts engaged in textual analysis."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 30" name="FRopinion1.30" href="#FNopinion1.30">30</a></sup>  Under <em>Auer</em>, judges are forced to subordinate their own views about what the law means to those of a political actor, one who may even be a party to the litigation before the court.  After all, if the court agrees that the agency's reading is the best one, <em>Auer</em> does no real work; the doctrine matters only when a court would conclude that the agency's interpretation is <em>not</em> the best or fairest reading of the regulation.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To be sure, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span> paints a very different picture of <em>Auer</em>, asking us to imagine it riding to the rescue only in cases where the scales of justice are evenly balanced between two equally persuasive readings.  But that's a fantasy: "If nature knows of such equipoise in legal arguments, the courts at least do not."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 31" name="FRopinion1.31" href="#FNopinion1.31">31</a></sup>  In the real world the judge uses his traditional interpretive toolkit, full of canons and tiebreaking rules, to reach a decision about the best and fairest reading of the law.  Of course, there are close cases and reasonable judges will sometimes disagree.  But every day, in courts throughout this country, judges manage with these traditional tools to reach conclusions about the meaning of statutes, rules of procedure, contracts, and the Constitution.  Yet when it comes to interpreting federal regulations, <em>Auer</em> displaces this process and requires judges instead to treat the agency's interpretation as controlling even when it is "not .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. the best one."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 32" name="FRopinion1.32" href="#FNopinion1.32">32</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;If that were not troubling enough, <em>Auer</em> has also become "a doctrine of uncertain scope and application."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 33" name="FRopinion1.33" href="#FNopinion1.33">33</a></sup>  This Court has never offered meaningful guidance on how to decide whether the agency's reading is "reasonable" enough to demand judicial deference--and lower courts have drawn that line in wildly different places.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 34" name="FRopinion1.34" href="#FNopinion1.34">34</a></sup>  Deepening the confusion, this Court and lower courts have, over time, tried to soften <em>Auer</em>'s rigidity by declaring that it "might" not apply in some ill-defined circumstances, such as when the agency's interpretation "conflicts with a prior interpretation" or reflects a "convenient litigating position" or a "<em>post&nbsp;hoc</em> rationalization" for past agency action.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 35" name="FRopinion1.35" href="#FNopinion1.35">35</a></sup>  All this has resulted in "widespread confusion" about when and how to apply <em>Auer</em> deference.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 36" name="FRopinion1.36" href="#FNopinion1.36">36</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In light of <em>Auer</em>'s many problems, it should come as no surprise that several Members of this Court,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 37" name="FRopinion1.37" href="#FNopinion1.37">37</a></sup> along with a great many lower court judges<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 38" name="FRopinion1.38" href="#FNopinion1.38">38</a></sup> and members of the legal academy,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 39" name="FRopinion1.39" href="#FNopinion1.39">39</a></sup> have questioned <em>Auer</em>'s validity and pleaded with this Court to reconsider it.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">D</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That's where things stood when James Kisor asked the Department of Veterans Affairs to reopen his disability benefits claim.  Mr. Kisor served as a United States Marine from 1962 through 1966 and saw combat in Vietnam.  In the early 1980s, a VA counselor observed that Mr. Kisor was battling depression and suicidal thoughts and suggested he might be suffering from post-traumatic stress disorder.  In light of this, Mr. Kisor filed a claim for disability benefits in 1982.  But, in the end, the VA denied the claim.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In 2006, Mr. Kisor sought to reopen the matter.  In connection with that request, he presented new evidence, including a psychiatrist's report diagnosing him with PTSD and additional records documenting his service in Vietnam.  The VA reopened Mr. Kisor's claim and granted him disability benefits effective June 5, 2006, the date he had submitted his new request.  Mr. Kisor argued that a VA regulation entitled him to an earlier effective date for disability benefits, one tracing back to his original submission in 1982.  But the Board of Veterans Appeals concluded that the applicable regulation didn't authorize that relief.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Mr. Kisor appealed the Board's ruling all the way to the Federal Circuit, arguing that the Board had misinterpreted the relevant regulation.  The Federal Circuit affirmed.  Relying on the <em>Auer</em> doctrine, the court held that it had no choice but to treat the Board's interpretation as "&nbsp;'controlling'&nbsp;" unless that interpretation was "&nbsp;'plainly erroneous or inconsistent with the regulatio[n].'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 40" name="FRopinion1.40" href="#FNopinion1.40">40</a></sup>  Without even trying to determine who had the better reading of the regulation, the Board or Mr. Kisor, the court declared that "[t]he Board's interpretation does not strike us as either plainly erroneous or inconsistent with the VA's regulatory framework."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 41" name="FRopinion1.41" href="#FNopinion1.41">41</a></sup>  Case closed.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Mr. Kisor sought and was denied rehearing en banc.  Three judges dissented and joined those who have questioned "the logic behind continued adherence to the [<em>Auer</em>] doctrine"; they argued that, without <em>Auer</em> deference, Mr. Kisor's reading of the regulation would likely prevail.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 42" name="FRopinion1.42" href="#FNopinion1.42">42</a></sup>  Mr. Kisor then asked us to grant certiorari to reconsider <em>Auer</em>.  Thinking it past time to do so, we granted the  petition.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 43" name="FRopinion1.43" href="#FNopinion1.43">43</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">II. The Administrative Procedure Act</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When this Court speaks about the rules governing judicial review of federal agency action, we are not (or shouldn't be) writing on a blank slate or exercising some common-law-making power.  We are supposed to be applying the Administrative Procedure Act.  The APA is a "seminal" statute that Congress wrote to define the relationship between courts and agencies.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 44" name="FRopinion1.44" href="#FNopinion1.44">44</a></sup>  Some have even described it as a kind of constitution for our "administrative state."  Yet, remarkably, until today this Court has never made any serious effort to square the <em>Auer</em> doctrine with the APA.  Even now, only four Justices make the attempt.  And for at least two reasons, their arguments are wholly unpersuasive.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The first problem lies in §706.  That provision instructs reviewing courts to "decide all relevant questions of law" and "set aside agency action .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. found to be .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. not in accordance with law."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 45" name="FRopinion1.45" href="#FNopinion1.45">45</a></sup>  Determining the meaning of a statute or regulation, of course, presents a classic legal question.  But in case these directives were not clear enough, the APA further directs courts to "determine the meaning" of any relevant "agency action," including any rule issued by the agency.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 46" name="FRopinion1.46" href="#FNopinion1.46">46</a></sup>  The APA thus requires a reviewing court to resolve for itself any dispute over the proper interpretation of an agency regulation.  A court that, in deference to an agency, adopts something other than the best reading of a regulation isn't "decid[ing]" the relevant "questio[n] of law" or "determin[ing] the meaning" of the regulation.  Instead, it's allowing the agency to dictate the answer to that question.  In doing so, the court is abdicating the duty Congress assigned to it in the APA.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 47" name="FRopinion1.47" href="#FNopinion1.47">47</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span> seeks to address the glaring inconsistency between our judge-made rule and the controlling statute this way.  On her account, the APA tells a reviewing court to "determine the meaning" of regulations, but it does not tell the court "<em>how</em>" to do that.  Thus, we are told, reading the regulation for itself and deferring to the agency's reading are just two equally valid ways for a court to fulfill its statutory duty to "determine the meaning" of the regulation.  <em>Ante</em>, at 20-21.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But the APA isn't as anemic as that.  Its unqualified command requires the court to determine legal questions--including questions about a regulation's meaning--by its own lights, not by those of political appointees or bureaucrats who may even be self-interested litigants in the case at hand.  Nor can there be any doubt that, when Congress wrote the APA, it knew perfectly well how to require judicial deference to an agency when it wished--in fact, Congress repeatedly specified deferential standards for judicial review <em>elsewhere</em> in the statute.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 48" name="FRopinion1.48" href="#FNopinion1.48">48</a></sup>  But when it comes to the business of interpreting regulations, no such command exists; instead, Congress told courts to "determine" those matters for themselves.  Though one hardly needs to be an academic to recognize the point, "commentators in administrative law have 'generally acknowledged' that Section 706 seems to require de&nbsp;novo review on questions of law."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 49" name="FRopinion1.49" href="#FNopinion1.49">49</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;What the statutory language suggests, experience confirms.  If <em>Auer</em> deference were really just another way for courts to "determine the meaning" of regulations under §706, you might expect that a final judicial "determination" would at least settle, as a matter of precedent, the question of what the regulation "means."  Of course, even after one court has spoken on a regulation's meaning, that court or another might properly give weight to a new agency interpretation as part of the court's own decision-making process.  See <em>supra</em>, at 6.  But in light of <em>National Cable &amp; Telecommunications Assn.</em> v. <em>Brand X Internet Services</em>,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 50" name="FRopinion1.50" href="#FNopinion1.50">50</a></sup> courts have interpreted <em>Auer</em> as forbidding a court from ever "determin[ing] the meaning" of a regulation with the force that normally attaches to precedent, because an agency is always free to adopt a different view and insist on judicial deference to its new judgment.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 51" name="FRopinion1.51" href="#FNopinion1.51">51</a></sup>  And if an agency can not only control the court's initial decision but also revoke that decision at any time, how can anyone honestly say the court, rather than the agency, ever really "determine[s]" what the regulation means?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To test the point further, consider a statute that tells a court to "determin[e]" an appropriate sentence in a criminal case.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 52" name="FRopinion1.52" href="#FNopinion1.52">52</a></sup>  If the judge said he was sending a defendant to prison for longer than he believed appropriate only in deference to the government's "reasonable" sentencing recommendation, would anyone really think that complied with the law?  Or take a statute that instructs a court to "determine" whether a consent judgment proposed by the government in a civil antitrust case "is in the public interest."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 53" name="FRopinion1.53" href="#FNopinion1.53">53</a></sup>  If a court thought the proposed judgment harmful to the public but decided to defer to the government's "reasonable" contrary view anyway, would anyone suggest the court had complied with Congress's instruction?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor does <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span>'s reading of §706 offer any logical stopping point.  If courts can "determine the meaning" of a regulation by deferring to any "reasonable" agency reading, then why not by deferring to <em>any</em> agency reading?  If it were really true that the APA has nothing to say about <em>how</em> courts decide what regulations mean, then it would follow that the APA tolerates a rule that "the agency is always right."  And if you find yourself in a place as absurd as that, you might want to consider whether you've taken a wrong turn along the way.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The problems don't end there.  <em>Auer</em> is also incompatible with the APA's instructions in §553.  That provision requires agencies to follow notice-and-comment procedures when issuing or amending legally binding regulations (what the APA calls "substantive rules"), but not when offering mere interpretations of those regulations.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 54" name="FRopinion1.54" href="#FNopinion1.54">54</a></sup>  An agency wishing to adopt or amend a binding regulation thus must publish a proposal in the Federal Register, give interested members of the public an opportunity to submit written comments on the proposal, and consider those comments before issuing the final regulation.  Under the APA, that regulation then carries the force of law unless and until it is amended or repealed.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 55" name="FRopinion1.55" href="#FNopinion1.55">55</a></sup>  By contrast, an agency can announce an interpretation of an existing substantive regulation without advance warning and in pretty much whatever form it chooses.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Auer</em> effectively nullifies the distinction Congress drew here.  Under <em>Auer</em>, courts must treat as "controlling" not only an agency's duly promulgated rules but also its mere interpretations--even ones that appear only in a legal brief, press release, or guidance document issued without affording the public advance notice or a chance to comment.  For all practical purposes, "the new interpretation might as well be a new regulation."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 56" name="FRopinion1.56" href="#FNopinion1.56">56</a></sup>  <em>Auer</em> thus obliterates a distinction Congress thought vital and supplies agencies with a shortcut around the APA's required procedures for issuing and amending substantive rules that bind the public with the full force and effect of law.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 57" name="FRopinion1.57" href="#FNopinion1.57">57</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Think of it this way.  We've held that the Constitution's specification of a "single, finely wrought" procedure for the enactment of statutes (bicameralism and presentment) necessarily implies that Congress cannot amend an enacted statute without following that procedure--say, by allowing a single House to change what the law requires.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 58" name="FRopinion1.58" href="#FNopinion1.58">58</a></sup>  By the same logic, Congress's specification in the APA of procedures for the creation of new substantive rules (like notice and comment) necessarily implies that an agency cannot amend a substantive rule without following those procedures.  To hold otherwise, as <em>Auer </em>demands, subverts the APA's design.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Certain <em>amici</em> contend this argument is "out of place" in this particular case because the VA happened to issue the interpretation challenged here in an adjudicative proceeding.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 59" name="FRopinion1.59" href="#FNopinion1.59">59</a></sup>  But the premise on which they proceed--that the APA permits agencies to issue "controlling" amendments to their regulations in adjudicative proceedings--is not correct.  Once an agency issues a substantive rule through notice and comment, it can amend that rule only by following the same notice-and-comment procedures.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 60" name="FRopinion1.60" href="#FNopinion1.60">60</a></sup>  Whether an agency issues its interpretation in a press release or something it chooses to call an "adjudication," all we have is the agency's opinion about what an existing rule means, something that the APA tells us is <em>not</em> binding in a court of law or on the American people.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;If that won't work, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span> tries an alternative argument from nearly the opposite direction.  She replies that affording <em>Auer</em> deference to an agency's interpretation of its own rules never offends the APA because the agency's interpretation lacks "the force of law" associated with substantive rules.  Agency interpretations lack this force, we are told, because a court always retains the power to decide at least whether the interpretation is entitled to deference.  <em>Ante</em>, at 22-23.  But this argument rests on an implausibly narrow understanding of what it means for an agency action to bear the force of law.  Under <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span>'s logic, even a binding substantive rule would lack the force of law because a court retains the power to decide whether the rule is arbitrary and capricious and thus invalid under the APA.  But no one believes that.  While an agency interpretation, just like a substantive rule, "must meet certain conditions before it gets deference," "once it does so [<em>Auer</em> makes it] every bit as binding as a substantive rule."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 61" name="FRopinion1.61" href="#FNopinion1.61">61</a></sup>  To suggest that <em>Auer</em> does not make an agency's interpretive guidance "binding o[n] anyone," <em>ante</em>, at 23, is linguistic hocus-pocus.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">C</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;If <em>Auer</em> cannot be squared with the text of the APA, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span> suggests it at least conforms to a reasonable "presumption about congressional intent."  <em>Ante</em>, at 7.  The theory seems to be that whenever Congress grants an agency "rulemaking power," it <em>also</em> implicitly gives the agency "&nbsp;'the power authoritatively to interpret'&nbsp;" whatever rules the agency chooses to adopt.  <em>Ante</em>, at 8.  But<em> </em>against the clear statutory commands Congress gave us in the APA, what sense does it make to "presume" that Congress really, secretly, wanted courts to treat agency interpretations as binding?  Normally, this Court does not allow hidden legislative intentions to "muddy" such plainly expressed statutory directives.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 62" name="FRopinion1.62" href="#FNopinion1.62">62</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Even on its own terms, too, this argument proves pretty muddy.  It goes something like this: The drafters of the APA did not intend to "&nbsp;'significantly alter'&nbsp;" established law governing judicial review of agency action as of 1946; the <em>Auer</em> doctrine was part of that established law; therefore, the APA implicitly requires courts to afford agencies <em>Auer</em> deference.  <em>Ante</em>, at 21-22.  But neither of this syllogism's essential premises stands on solid ground.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Take the major premise--that those who adopted the APA intended to work no change in the established law of judicial review of agency action.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span> is right, of course, that Attorney General Clark claimed as much shortly after the APA's passage.  <em>Ante</em>, at 21.  But his view, which reflected the interests of the executive branch, was far from universally shared.  Others, including many members of Congress, thought the APA would clarify, if not expand, the scope of judicial review.  For example, Senator McCarran, the Chairman of the Judiciary Committee, wrote that it would be "hard .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. for anyone to argue that this Act did anything other than cut down the 'cult of discretion' so far as federal law is concerned."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 63" name="FRopinion1.63" href="#FNopinion1.63">63</a></sup>  And both the House and Senate reports on the APA said it was intended to "provid[e] that questions of law are for courts rather than agencies to decide in the last analysis."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 64" name="FRopinion1.64" href="#FNopinion1.64">64</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Just five years after the APA's passage, this Court seemed to side with those who thought the APA was intended to do more than just summarize existing law.  In an opinion by Justice Frankfurter, the Court opined that the APA required courts to assume "<em>more</em> responsibility" for reviewing agency decisions "than some courts ha[d] shown in the past."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 65" name="FRopinion1.65" href="#FNopinion1.65">65</a></sup>  One early commentator likewise observed that the APA seemed designed to eliminate all doubt that questions of law "shall be decided by the reviewing Court for itself, and in the exercise of its own independent judgment"; "[m]ore explicit words to impose this mandate," he thought, "could hardly be found."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 66" name="FRopinion1.66" href="#FNopinion1.66">66</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span>'s syllogism runs into even more trouble with its minor premise--that the <em>Auer</em> doctrine was a well-established part of the common law background when Congress enacted the APA in 1946.  As we've seen, this Court planted the seeds of <em>Auer</em> deference for the first time in dictum in <em>Seminole Rock</em>, just a year before Congress passed the APA.  See Part I-B, <em>supra</em>.  And that dictum did not somehow immediately become an entrenched part of the common law: For years following <em>Seminole Rock</em>, courts and "commentators largely ignored" it,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 67" name="FRopinion1.67" href="#FNopinion1.67">67</a></sup> and those who took notice weren't sure what to make of it.  Professor Davis, for example, doubted that the dictum could be "taken at face value" given that it seemed "irreconcilable" with the Court's approach in other cases.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 68" name="FRopinion1.68" href="#FNopinion1.68">68</a></sup>  In truth, when Congress passed the APA the law of judicial review of agency action was in a confused state.  During the congressional hearings on the bill, one witness's suggestion that Congress should leave the scope of judicial review "as it now is" drew this fair reply from Representative Walter, chairman of the House Subcommittee on Administrative Law and author of the House Report on the APA: "You say 'as it now is.'  Frankly, I do not know what it now is .&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.  [T]he Supreme Court apparently changes its mind daily."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 69" name="FRopinion1.69" href="#FNopinion1.69">69</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">III. The Constitution</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Not only is <em>Auer</em> incompatible with the APA; it also sits uneasily with the Constitution.  Article III, §1 provides that the "judicial Power of the United States" is vested exclusively in this Court and the lower federal courts.  A core component of that judicial power is "&nbsp;'the duty of interpreting [the laws] and applying them in cases properly brought before the courts.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 70" name="FRopinion1.70" href="#FNopinion1.70">70</a></sup>  As Chief Justice Marshall put it, "[i]t is emphatically the province and duty of the judicial department to say what the law is."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 71" name="FRopinion1.71" href="#FNopinion1.71">71</a></sup>  And never, this Court has warned, should the "judicial power .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. be shared with [the] Executive Branch."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 72" name="FRopinion1.72" href="#FNopinion1.72">72</a></sup>  Yet that seems to be exactly what <em>Auer</em> requires.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Our Nation's founders were painfully aware of the dangers of executive and legislative intrusion on judicial decision-making.  One of the abuses of royal power that led to the American Revolution was King George's attempt to gain influence over colonial judges.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 73" name="FRopinion1.73" href="#FNopinion1.73">73</a></sup>  Colonial legislatures, too, had interfered with the courts' independence "at the behest of private interests and factions."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 74" name="FRopinion1.74" href="#FNopinion1.74">74</a></sup>  These experiences had taught the founders that "&nbsp;'there is no liberty if the power of judgment be not separated from the legislative and executive powers.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 75" name="FRopinion1.75" href="#FNopinion1.75">75</a></sup>  They knew that when political actors are left free not only to adopt and enforce written laws, but also to control the interpretation of those laws, the legal rights of "litigants with unpopular or minority causes or .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. who belong to despised or suspect classes" count for little.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 76" name="FRopinion1.76" href="#FNopinion1.76">76</a></sup>  Maybe the powerful, well-heeled, popular, and connected can wheedle favorable outcomes from a system like that--but what about everyone else?  They are left always a little unsure what the law is, at the mercy of political actors and the shifting winds of popular opinion, and without the chance for a fair hearing before a neutral judge.  The rule of law begins to bleed into the rule of men.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Experiencing all this in their own time, the founders sought to ensure that those who came after them would not.  Believing that "[n]o maxim was better established" than "that the power of making ought to be kept distinct from that of expounding, the laws,"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 77" name="FRopinion1.77" href="#FNopinion1.77">77</a></sup> they designed a judiciary that would be able to interpret the laws "free from potential domination by other branches of government."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 78" name="FRopinion1.78" href="#FNopinion1.78">78</a></sup>  To that end, they resisted proposals that would have subjected judicial decisions to review by political actors.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 79" name="FRopinion1.79" href="#FNopinion1.79">79</a></sup>  And they rejected the British tradition of using the upper house of the legislature as a court of last resort, out of fear that a body with "even a partial agency in passing bad laws" would operate under the "same spirit" in "interpreting them."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 80" name="FRopinion1.80" href="#FNopinion1.80">80</a></sup>  Instead, they gave federal judges life tenure, subject only to removal by impeachment; and they guaranteed that the other branches could not reduce judges' compensation so long as they remained in office.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The founders afforded these extraordinary powers and protections not for the comfort of judges, but so that an independent judiciary could better guard the people from the arbitrary use of governmental power.  And sitting atop the judicial branch, this Court has always carried a special duty to "jealously guar[d]" the Constitution's promise of judicial independence.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 81" name="FRopinion1.81" href="#FNopinion1.81">81</a></sup>  So we have long resisted any effort by the other branches to "&nbsp;'usurp a court's power to interpret and apply the law to the circumstances before it.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 82" name="FRopinion1.82" href="#FNopinion1.82">82</a></sup>  The judicial power to interpret the law, this Court has held, "can no more be shared with another branch than the Chief Executive, for example, can share with the Judiciary the veto power, or the Congress share with the Judiciary the power to override a Presidential veto."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 83" name="FRopinion1.83" href="#FNopinion1.83">83</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Auer</em> represents no trivial threat to these foundational principles.  Under the APA, substantive rules issued by federal agencies through notice-and-comment procedures bear "the 'force and effect of law'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 84" name="FRopinion1.84" href="#FNopinion1.84">84</a></sup> and are part of the body of federal law, binding on private individuals, that the Constitution charges federal judges with interpreting.  Yet <em>Auer</em> tells the judge that he must interpret these binding laws to mean not what he thinks they mean, but what an executive agency says they mean.  Unlike Article III judges, executive officials are not, nor are they supposed to be, "wholly impartial."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 85" name="FRopinion1.85" href="#FNopinion1.85">85</a></sup>  They have their own interests, their own constituencies, and their own policy goals--and when interpreting a regulation, they may choose to "press the case for the side [they] represen[t]" instead of adopting the fairest and best reading.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 86" name="FRopinion1.86" href="#FNopinion1.86">86</a></sup>  <em>Auer</em> thus means that, far from being "kept distinct," the powers of making, enforcing, and interpreting laws are united in the same hands--and in the process a cornerstone of the rule of law is compromised.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Consider an analogy.  The Court has long held that Congress cannot "&nbsp;'indirectly control the action of the courts, by requiring of them a construction of the law according to its own views.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 87" name="FRopinion1.87" href="#FNopinion1.87">87</a></sup>  If Congress disagrees with how courts are interpreting an existing statute, it is free to amend the statute to establish a different rule going forward.  What it cannot do is issue "a mandate .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. to compel the courts to construe and apply [existing law], not according to the judicial, but according to the legislative judgment."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 88" name="FRopinion1.88" href="#FNopinion1.88">88</a></sup>  As early as 1804, when a lawyer argued before this Court that an Act of the North Carolina legislature could not control the Court's construction of an earlier North Carolina statute because "[t]o declare what the law is, or has been, is a judicial power," not a legislative power, the Court stopped him, deeming the point too plain for argument.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 89" name="FRopinion1.89" href="#FNopinion1.89">89</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But if the legislature can't control a judge's interpretation of an existing statute, how can an executive agency control a judge's interpretation of an existing and equally binding regulation?  <em>Auer</em> allows an agency to do exactly what this Court has always said a legislature cannot do: "compel the courts to construe and apply" a law on the books, "not according to the judicial .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. judgment," but according to the judgment of another branch.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 90" name="FRopinion1.90" href="#FNopinion1.90">90</a></sup>  When we defer to an agency interpretation that differs from what we believe to be the best interpretation of the law, we compromise our judicial independence and deny the people who come before us the impartial judgment that the Constitution guarantees them.  And we mislead those whom we serve by placing a judicial <em>imprimatur</em> on what is, in fact, no more than an exercise of raw political executive power.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 91" name="FRopinion1.91" href="#FNopinion1.91">91</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;What do our colleagues have to say about these concerns?  A majority has nothing to offer, and<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small> Justice Kagan</small></span> dismisses them out of hand.  In fact, she barely mentions the Constitution, other than to assure us that <em>Auer</em> does not allow agencies to "usur[p] the interpretive role of courts" because "courts retain a firm grip on the interpretive function" through their ability to decide whether <em>Auer</em> deference applies.  <em>Ante</em>, at 25.  But that is no assurance at all.  The judicial power has always been understood to provide the people with a neutral arbiter who bears the responsibility and duty to "expound  and interpret" the governing law, not just the power to  say whether <em>someone else's</em> interpretation, let alone the interpretation of a self-interested political actor, is  "reasonable."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 92" name="FRopinion1.92" href="#FNopinion1.92">92</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To be sure, it's conceivable that Congress might seek to limit the ability of judges to remedy an adverse agency action.  It might, for example, provide that a court shall have power to set aside agency action pursuant to a regulation only if the action was based on an unreasonable interpretation of the regulation.  But even assuming the constitutionality of a hypothetical statute like that, <em>Auer</em> is different.  It does not <em>limit</em> the scope of the judicial power; instead, it seeks to <em>coopt</em> the judicial power by requiring an Article III judge to decide a case before him according to principles that he believes do not accurately reflect the law.  Under <em>Auer</em>, a judge is required to lay aside his independent judgment and declare affirmatively that a regulation <em>means</em> what the agency <em>says</em> it means--and, thus, that the law <em>is</em> what the agency <em>says</em> it is.  Then the judge is compelled to exercise his judicial authority to adjust private rights and obligations based on the agency's (mis)understanding of the law.  If <em>Auer</em> were a statute, it would not be an exercise of Congress's "power (within limits) to tell the courts what <em>classes</em> of cases they may decide," or what relief they may supply, but a forbidden attempt "to prescribe or superintend <em>how</em> they decide those cases."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 93" name="FRopinion1.93" href="#FNopinion1.93">93</a></sup>  And in the absence of any statute like that, this Court surely should not so freely give away to the executive branch its assigned responsibility to interpret the laws.  "Abdication of responsibility is not part of the constitutional design."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 94" name="FRopinion1.94" href="#FNopinion1.94">94</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In the end, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span>'s only real reply is this: However misguided it may be to hand over our interpretive powers to executive agencies, at least there isn't a mountain of empirical evidence showing that agencies have used this power to deliberately write "vague and open-ended" regulations to maximize their interpretive leeway.  <em>Ante</em>, at 24.  But even this misses the point.  Whether or not regulations are "&nbsp;'designed'&nbsp;" to be vague, <em>ibid.</em>, many can be read in different ways, especially when new and unanticipated applications arise; cases like that come before the courts all the time.  Without <em>Auer</em>'s shadow hanging over them, parties would receive a fair hearing before an impartial judge.  The agency's interpretation would sometimes be rejected; and that, in turn, might lead it to solicit public comment on possible amendments to the regulation, which would provide an opportunity for public input that might produce better policy.  But with <em>Auer</em>, there is no fair hearing and no need for the agency to amend the regulation through notice and comment.  Whether purposeful or not, the agency's failure to write a clear regulation winds up increasing its power, allowing it to both write and interpret rules that bear the force of law--in the process uniting powers the Constitution deliberately separated and denying the people their right to an independent judicial determination of the law's meaning.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">IV. Policy Arguments</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Lacking support elsewhere, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span> is forced to resort to policy arguments to defend <em>Auer</em>.  But even the most sensible policy argument would not empower us to ignore the plain language of the APA or the demands of the Constitution.  And as we've seen, those documents reflect a very different "policy" judgment by the people  and their representatives.  Besides, the policy arguments offered today are not just unpersuasive, they are troubling.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Take the first and boldest offering.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span> suggests that determining the meaning of a regulation is largely a matter of figuring out what the "person who wrote it .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. intended."  <em>Ante</em>, at 8.  In this way, we're told, a legally binding regulation isn't all that different from "a memo or an e-mail"--if you "[w]ant to know what [it] means," you'd better "[a]sk its author."  <em>Ante</em>, at 8-9.  But the federal government's substantive rules are not like memos or e-mails; they are binding edicts that carry the force of law for all citizens.  And if the rule of law means anything, it means that we are governed by the public meaning of the words found in statutes and regulations, not by their authors' private intentions.  This is a vital part of what it means to have "a government of laws, and not of men."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 95" name="FRopinion1.95" href="#FNopinion1.95">95</a></sup>  When judges interpret a regulation, what we are trying to get at, as Justice Holmes explained long ago, is not the "particular intent" of those who wrote it, but "what [its] words would mean [to] a normal speaker of English .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. in the circumstances in which they were used."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 96" name="FRopinion1.96" href="#FNopinion1.96">96</a></sup>  If the best reading of the regulation turns out to be something other than what the agency claims to have intended, the agency is free to rewrite the regulation; but its secret intentions are not the law.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor does <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span>'s account of the interpretive process even wind up supporting <em>Auer</em>.  If a court's goal in interpreting a regulation really were to determine what its author "intended," <em>Auer</em> would be an almost complete mismatch with the goal.  Agency personnel change over time, and an agency's policy priorities may shift dramatically from one presidential administration to another.  Yet <em>Auer</em> tells courts that they must defer to the agency's <em>current</em> view of what the regulation ought to mean, which may or may not correspond to the views of those who actually wrote it.  If interpreting a regulation really were just like reading an e-mail, <em>Auer</em> would be like seeking guidance about the e-mail's meaning, years or decades later, from the latest user of the computer from which the e-mail was sent.  We've repeatedly rejected that approach in the context of statutory interpretation.  While Members of this Court sometimes disagree about the usefulness of <em>pre-enactment</em> legislative history, we all agree that legislators' statements about the meaning of an already-enacted statute are not "a legitimate tool of statutory interpretation,'&nbsp;" much less a controlling one.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 97" name="FRopinion1.97" href="#FNopinion1.97">97</a></sup>  So why on earth would we give "controlling weight" to an agency's statements about the meaning of an already-promulgated regulation?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Proceeding farther down this doubtful path, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span> asserts that resolving ambiguities in a regulation "sounds more in policy than in law" and is thus a task more suited to executive officials than judges.  <em>Ante</em>, at 9.  But this claim, too, contradicts a basic premise of our legal order: that we are governed not by the shifting whims of politicians and bureaucrats, but by written laws whose meaning is fixed and ascertainable--if not by all members of the public, then at least by lawyers who can advise them and judges who must apply the law to individual cases guided by the neutral principles found in our traditional tools of interpretation.  The text of the regulation is treated <em>as</em> the law, and the agency's policy judgment has the force of law <em>only</em> insofar as it is embodied in the regulatory text.  If "new issues demanding new policy calls" arise that aren't addressed in existing regulations, <em>ante</em>, at 10, the solution is for the agency to promulgate new regulations using the notice-and-comment procedures set forth in the APA.  But an agency has no warrant to compel judges to change the law to conform with the agency's current policy preferences.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To be sure, during the period of <em>Auer</em>'s ascendancy some suggested that the meaning of written law is always "radically indeterminate" and that judges expounding it are "for the most part, guided by policy--not text."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 98" name="FRopinion1.98" href="#FNopinion1.98">98</a></sup>  And in an environment like that it was perhaps thought a small step to conclude that, if legal disputes are going to be resolved on political grounds, then they ought to be resolved by real politicians in the executive branch rather than ersatz politicians on the bench.  But the proposed cure proved worse than the disease.  Arguments like these surrendered the judgment embodied in our Constitution and the APA that courts owe the people they serve their independent legal judgment about the law's meaning.  Besides, we've long since come to realize that the real cure doesn't lie in turning judges into rubber stamps for politicians, but in redirecting the judge's interpretive task back to its roots, away from open-ended policy appeals and speculation about legislative intentions and toward the traditional tools of interpretation judges have employed for centuries to elucidate the law's original public meaning.  Today it is even said that we judges are, to one degree or another, "all textualists now."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 99" name="FRopinion1.99" href="#FNopinion1.99">99</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Pursuing a more modest tack, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span> next suggests that <em>Auer</em> is justified by the respect due agencies' "technical" expertise.  <em>Ante</em>, at 10.  But no one doubts that courts should pay close attention to an expert agency's views on technical questions in its field.  Just as a court "would want to know what John Henry Wigmore said about an issue of evidence law [or] what Arthur Corbin thought about a matter of contract law," so too should courts carefully consider what the Food and Drug Administration thinks about how its prescription drug safety regulations operate.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 100" name="FRopinion1.100" href="#FNopinion1.100">100</a></sup>  The fact remains, however, that even agency experts "can be wrong; even Homer nodded."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 101" name="FRopinion1.101" href="#FNopinion1.101">101</a></sup>  <em>Skidmore</em> and the traditional approach it embodied recognized both of these facts of life long ago, explaining that, while courts should of course afford respectful consideration to the expert agency's views, they must remain open to competing expert and other evidence supplied in an adversarial setting.  Respect for an agency's technical expertise demands no more.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span>'s final policy argument is that <em>Auer</em> promotes "consistency" and "uniformity" in the interpretation of regulations.  <em>Ante</em>, at 10-11.  If we let courts decide what regulations mean, she warns, they might disagree, and it might take some time for higher courts to resolve those disagreements.  But consistency and uniformity are hardly grounds on which <em>Auer</em>'s advocates should wish to fight.  The judicial process is how we settle disputes about the meaning of written law, and our judicial system is more than capable of producing a single, uniform, and stable interpretation that will last until the regulation is amended or repealed.  Meanwhile, under <em>Auer</em> courts often disagree about whether deference is warranted, see <em>supra</em>, at 10-11, and a regulation's "meaning" can be transformed with the stroke of a pen any time there is a new presidential administration.  "Consistency," "uniformity," and stability in the law are hardly among <em>Auer</em>'s crowning achievements.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">V. <em>Stare Decisis</em></p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In the end, a majority declines to endorse <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kagan</small></span>'s arguments and insists only that, even if <em>Auer</em> is not "right and well-reasoned," we're stuck with it because of the respect due precedent.  <em>Ante</em>, at 27.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But notice: While pretending to bow to <em>stare decisis</em>, the majority goes about reshaping our precedent in new and experimental ways.  True, the majority admits, this Court has in the past accorded <em>Auer</em> deference "&nbsp;'reflexive[ly],'&nbsp;" "without significant analysis of the underlying regulation" or "careful attention to [its] nature and context," and encouraged lower courts to do the same.  <em>Ante</em>, at 12-13.  But no more.  From now on, the majority says, not only must judges "exhaust all the 'traditional tools' of construction" to decide whether the agency's interpretation is "reasonable," they must also make "an independent inquiry into whether the character and context of the agency interpretation" justifies deference.  <em>Ante</em>, at 13-15.  The majority candidly admits that it finds it impossible to "reduce" this new inquiry "to any exhaustive test," so it settles for laying out some "markers."  <em>Ante</em>, at 15.  What are the markers?  We are told that courts should often--but not always--withhold deference from an interpretation offered by mid-level agency staff; often--but not always--withhold deference from a nontechnical, "prosaic-seeming" interpretation; often--but not always--withhold deference from an interpretation advanced for the first time in an <em>amicus</em> brief; and often--but not always--withhold deference from an interpretation that conflicts with an earlier one.  See <em>ante</em>, at 15-18.  The only certainty in all this is that the majority isn't really much moved by <em>stare decisis</em>; everyone recognizes, to one degree or another, that <em>Auer</em> cannot stand.  And between our remaining choices--continuing to make up new deference rules, or returning to the text of the APA and the approach to judicial review that prevailed for most of our history--the answer should have been easy.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;There are serious questions about whether <em>stare decisis</em> should apply here at all.  To be sure, <em>Auer</em>'s narrow holding about the meaning of the regulation at issue in that case may be entitled to <em>stare decisis</em> effect.  The same may be true for the specific holdings in other cases where this Court has applied <em>Auer</em> deference.  But does <em>stare decisis</em> extend beyond those discrete holdings and bind future Members of this Court to apply <em>Auer</em>'s broader deference framework?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It seems doubtful that <em>stare decisis</em> demands that much.  We are not dealing with a precedent that purported to settle the meaning of a single statute or regulation or resolve a particular case.  The <em>Auer</em> doctrine claims to do much more than that--to prescribe an interpretive methodology governing every future dispute over the meaning of every regulation.  In other contexts, we do not regard statements in our opinions about such generally applicable interpretive methods, like the proper weight to afford historical practice in constitutional cases or legislative history in statutory cases, as binding future Justices with the full force of horizontal <em>stare decisis</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 102" name="FRopinion1.102" href="#FNopinion1.102">102</a></sup>  Why, then, should we regard as binding <em>Auer</em>'s statements about the weight to afford agencies' interpretations in regulatory cases?  To the extent <em>Auer</em> purports to dictate "the interpretive inferences that future Justices must draw in construing statutes and regulations that the Court has never engaged," it may well "exceed the limits of stare decisis."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 103" name="FRopinion1.103" href="#FNopinion1.103">103</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Even if our past expressions of support for <em>Auer</em> deference bear <em>some</em> precedential force, they certainly are not entitled (as the majority suggests, <em>ante</em>, at 26-27) to the special, heightened form of <em>stare decisis</em> we reserve for narrow statutory decisions.  In contrast to precedents that fix the meaning of <em>particular</em> statutes and generate reliance interests in the process, the <em>Auer</em> doctrine is an abstract default rule of interpretive methodology that settles nothing of its own force.  And this Court has recognized that it is "inconsistent with the Court's proper role" to insist that Congress exercise its legislative power to overturn such erroneous and judicially invented "default rule[s]."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 104" name="FRopinion1.104" href="#FNopinion1.104">104</a></sup>  That should be especially so here because <em>Auer</em>'s default rule undermines judicial independence, which this Court has a special responsibility to defend.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor is it entirely clear that Congress <em>could</em> overturn the <em>Auer</em> doctrine legislatively.  The majority describes <em>Auer</em> as a "presumption" about how courts should interpret statutes granting rulemaking power to agencies.  <em>Ante</em>, at 12.  Congress can, of course, <em>rebut</em> the presumption on a statute-by-statute basis, or even for all past statutes.  But can Congress <em>eliminate</em> the <em>Auer</em> presumption for future statutes?  Perhaps--but legislation like that would raise questions, which the majority does not address, about the ability of one Congress to entrench its preferences by attempting to control the interpretation of legislation enacted by future Congresses.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 105" name="FRopinion1.105" href="#FNopinion1.105">105</a></sup>  We should not be in the business of tossing "&nbsp;'balls .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. into Congress's court,'&nbsp;" <em>ante</em>, at 27, that would explode with constitutional questions if Congress tried to pick them up.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Even assuming for argument's sake that standard <em>stare decisis</em> considerations apply, they still do not require us to retain <em>Auer</em>.  Even the majority implicitly recognizes this much, as it proceeds to vacate a lower court judgment that faithfully applied <em>Auer</em> and instruct that court to try again using the majority's new directions.  If <em>stare decisis</em> allows us so freely to remodel <em>Auer</em>, it's hard to see on what account it might require us to retain it.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We do not lightly overturn precedents, and we seek always to honor the thoughtful guidance of those who have preceded us.  At the same time, everyone agrees that <em>stare decisis</em> is not an "&nbsp;'inexorable command,'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 106" name="FRopinion1.106" href="#FNopinion1.106">106</a></sup> and this Court should not always remain bound to decisions whose "rationale no longer withstands 'careful analysis.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 107" name="FRopinion1.107" href="#FNopinion1.107">107</a></sup>  Recognizing the need for balance in this area, the Court has, over time, fashioned principles to guide our treatment of precedent.  Those principles call on us to consider factors such as "the quality of [the precedent's] reasoning, the workability of the rule it established, its consistency with other related decisions, developments since the decision was handed down, and reliance on the decision."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 108" name="FRopinion1.108" href="#FNopinion1.108">108</a></sup>  As applied to <em>Auer</em>, all of these considerations weigh strongly in favor of bidding farewell to the doctrine rather than keeping it on life support.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>First</em>, we've already seen that no persuasive rationale supports <em>Auer</em>.  From its humble origins as an unexplained bit of dictum in a wartime case about emergency price controls, the <em>Auer</em> doctrine evolved into a rigid rule of deference--all without any serious attempt by this Court to rationalize it or reconcile it with the APA, the Constitution, or traditional modes of judicial review.  See Part I, <em>supra</em>.  Even its fiercest defenders acknowledge that "<em>Auer</em> deference has not remained static over time" and urge the Court to continue to "shape" and "refin[e]" the doctrine.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 109" name="FRopinion1.109" href="#FNopinion1.109">109</a></sup>  Today's decision attempts just such a "refinement" by hedging <em>Auer</em> with new qualifications and limitations.  See <em>ante</em>, at 11-18.  This shifting ground "undermin[es] the force of <em>stare decisis</em>."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 110" name="FRopinion1.110" href="#FNopinion1.110">110</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Second</em>, today's ruling all but admits that <em>Auer</em> has not proved to be a workable standard.  Even before this latest overhaul, uncertainty surrounding <em>Auer</em>'s scope and application had caused many to question whether there was any "practical benefit" in continuing to apply <em>Auer</em> "rather than a less deferential but more flexible and open-ended standard like <em>Skidmore</em>."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 111" name="FRopinion1.111" href="#FNopinion1.111">111</a></sup>  See <em>supra</em>, at 10-11.  Nor does the majority's kinder, gentler version of <em>Auer</em> promise to solve the problem.  On the contrary, its newly mandated inquiry into the "character and context of the agency interpretation," which it admits cannot be reduced "to any exhaustive test," <em>ante</em>, at 15, seems destined only to compound the confusion.  See <em>supra</em>, at 35.  Many words come to mind to describe the tasks we assign lower court judges today, but "workable" is not among them.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Third</em>, the <em>Auer</em> doctrine is, as we have also already seen, out of step with how courts normally interpret written laws.  When we interpret a regulation, we typically (at least when there is no agency say-so) proceed in the same way we would when interpreting any other written law: We "begin our interpretation of the regulation with its text" and, if the text is unclear, we "turn to other canons of interpretation" and tie-breaking rules to resolve the ambiguity.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 112" name="FRopinion1.112" href="#FNopinion1.112">112</a></sup>  And when we interpret an ambiguous <em>statute</em>, we never ask what current members of Congress think it means; in fact, we've held unanimously that legislators' post-enactment views about a statute's meaning are not even a "&nbsp;'legitimate tool of statutory interpretation.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 113" name="FRopinion1.113" href="#FNopinion1.113">113</a></sup>  Affording "controlling weight" to regulators' post-promulgation views about the meaning of an ambiguous regulation is hard to square with these usual judicial practices.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 114" name="FRopinion1.114" href="#FNopinion1.114">114</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Fourth</em>, the explosive growth of the administrative state over the last half-century has exacerbated <em>Auer</em>'s potential for mischief.  When the Court first uttered its dictum in <em>Seminole Rock</em>, the administrative state was new and the APA was only a gleam in Congress's eye.  Even 20 years later, when the Court began reviving the <em>Seminole Rock</em> dictum and turning it into a new deference doctrine, it was not yet apparent how pervasive the administrative state would become in the lives of ordinary Americans.  Now, in the 21st century, "[t]he administrative state wields vast power and touches almost every aspect of daily life."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 115" name="FRopinion1.115" href="#FNopinion1.115">115</a></sup>  Among other things, it produces "&nbsp;'reams of regulations'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 116" name="FRopinion1.116" href="#FNopinion1.116">116</a></sup>--so many that they dwarf the statutes enacted by Congress.  As of 2018, the Code of Federal Regulations filled 242 volumes and was about 185,000 pages long, almost quadruple the length of the most recent edition of the U.&nbsp;S. Code.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 117" name="FRopinion1.117" href="#FNopinion1.117">117</a></sup>  And agencies add thousands more pages of regulations every year.  Whether you think this administrative fecundity is a good or a bad thing, it surely means that the cost of continuing to deny citizens an impartial judicial hearing on the meaning of disputed regulations has increased dramatically since this Court started down this road.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Fifth</em>, <em>Auer</em> has generated no serious reliance interests.  The only parties that might have relied on <em>Auer</em>'s promise of deference are agencies that use <em>post&nbsp;hoc</em> interpretations to bypass the APA's notice-and-comment procedures.  But this Court has never suggested that the convenience of government officials should count in the balance of <em>stare decisis</em>, especially when weighed against the interests of citizens in a fair hearing before an independent judge and a stable and knowable set of laws.  In short, "&nbsp;'[t]he fact that [agencies] may view [<em>Auer</em> deference] as an entitlement does not establish the sort of reliance interest that could outweigh the countervailing interest'&nbsp;" of all citizens "&nbsp;'in having their constitutional rights fully protected.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 118" name="FRopinion1.118" href="#FNopinion1.118">118</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Coming closer to the mark, the majority worries that "abandoning <em>Auer</em> deference would cast doubt on many settled constructions" of regulations on which regulated parties might have relied.  <em>Ante</em>, at 26.  But, again, decisions construing particular regulations might retain <em>stare decisis</em> effect even if the Court announced that it would no longer adhere to <em>Auer</em>'s interpretive methodology.  After all, decisions construing particular statutes continue to command respect even when the interpretive methods that led to those constructions fall out of favor.  Besides, if the majority is correct that abandoning <em>Auer</em> would require revisiting regulatory constructions that were upheld based on <em>Auer</em> deference, the majority's revision of <em>Auer</em> will yield exactly the same result.  There are innumerable lower court decisions that have followed this Court's lead and afforded <em>Auer</em> deference mechanically, without conducting the inquiry the Court now holds is required.  Today's ruling casts no less doubt on the continuing validity of those decisions than we would if we simply moved on from <em>Auer</em>.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">*</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Overruling <em>Auer</em> would have taken us directly back to <em>Skidmore</em>, liberating courts to decide cases based on their independent judgment and "follow [the] agency's [view] only to the extent it is persuasive."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 119" name="FRopinion1.119" href="#FNopinion1.119">119</a></sup>  By contrast, the majority's attempt to remodel <em>Auer</em>'s rule into a multi-step, multi-factor inquiry guarantees more uncertainty and much litigation.  Proceeding in this convoluted way burdens our colleagues on the lower courts, who will have to spend time debating deference that they could have spent interpreting disputed regulations.  It also continues to deny the people who come before us the neutral forum for their disputes that they rightly expect and deserve.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But this cloud may have a silver lining: The majority leaves <em>Auer</em> so riddled with holes that, when all is said and done, courts may find that it does not constrain their independent judgment any more than <em>Skidmore</em>.  As reengineered, <em>Auer </em>requires courts to "exhaust all the 'traditional tools' of construction" before they even consider deferring to an agency.  <em>Ante</em>, at 13-14.
